Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Hanafi Ruling on Wife's Maintenance When Husband is Unable: The Loan Remedy vs. Judicial Separation
Husband's inability to pay maintenance and the dispute over separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a wife's remedy when her husband fails to pay maintenance due to inability or absence, and compare it with the Shafi'i position, including the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A wife whose husband cannot pay maintenance should take a loan, not seek immediate separation; preserving the marriage outweighs temporary financial harm.

When a husband cannot provide maintenance due to poverty or absence, the wife may feel trapped. The Hanafi school, prioritizing marriage stability, offers a unique remedy: the wife can take a loan in the husband's name. This lesson explores the evidence behind that position and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view that immediate judicial separation is warranted.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi school's core ruling: when a husband is unable to pay maintenance, the wife is not entitled to immediate judicial separation; instead, she may take a loan (qard) in the husband's name to cover her needs.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the wife may obtain judicial separation (tafriq) immediately upon proof of the husband's inability to pay maintenance.
3
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Analyze the athar of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, who stated that separation is sunna. Consider the strength of this report as a precedent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Understand the Hanafi reliance on the athar of al-Hasan al-Basri ('separation is not made') and the hadith of Hind ('take what suffices'). Note how the loan remedy prevents greater harm (tafriq) from occurring.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Contrast the outcomes: under Shafi'i law, the wife can leave the marriage; under Hanafi law, she remains married while the loan becomes a debt on the husband. Recognize the later Hanafi concession when loans are impractical.
This thamarah al-khilaf affects the stability of the marriage and the wife's immediate options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النفقات علیه ۱۹۲۱۴۶) واذا غاب الرجل وله مال فى يد رجل يعترف به وبالزوجية فرض قرض لیتی رہے اور زندگی گزارتی رہے۔ تفریق کرنے سے شوہر کا نقصان ہے جو نفقہ نہ ادا کرنے سے زیادہ بڑا نقصان ہے۔ اس لئے تفریق نہیں کی جائے گی اور نفقہ کا نقصان قرض لینے سے پورا ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے عورت کو کوئی بڑا نقصان نہیں ہوا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن الحسن قال اذا عجز الرجل عن نفقة امرأته لم يفرق بها . وقال الزهرى تستأنى به ، قال وبلغنى ان عمر بن عبد العزيز قال ذلك (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی هیبتة ۱۹۷ ما قالوا فی الرجل يعجز عن نفقة امرأة يجبر على ان يطلق امراته ام لا داختلافهما فی ذلک ج رابع ، ص ۱۷۵، نمبر ۱۹۰۰۹/ ۱۹۰۰۸ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب الرجل لا يجد ما ينفق على امرأته ج سابع ص ۹۵ نمبر ۱۲۳۵۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ میاں بیوی میں تفریق نہ کرائے بلکہ عورت شوہر کے ذمے قرض لیتی رہے (۳) حضرت ابوسفیان کی بیوی کی حدیث بھی مستدل بن سکتی ہے جس میں حضرت ابوسفیان پورا نفقہ نہیں دیتے تھے تو آپ نے فرمایا۔ خذی مایکفیک و ولدک بالمعروف(ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب اذالم ينفق الرجل فللمرأة ان تأخذ بغير علمه ما يكفيها و ولدها بالمعروف ص ۸۰۸ نمبر ۵۳۶۴) امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ تفریق کر دی جائے گی۔ اثر میں ہے۔ سألت سعيد ابن المسيب عن الرجل يعجز عن نفقة امرأته فقال يفرق بينهما فقلت سنة ؟ فقال سنة (ج) (مصنف ابن ابی هیبتة ۱۹۷ ما قالوافی الرجل يعجز عن نطقه امراة يجبر على ايطلق امره ته ام لا واختلافهما فی ذلک ج رابع ص ۱۷۴، نمبر ۱۹۰۰۶ر مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل لا يجد ما يخفق على امرؤ ته ج سابع ص ۹۶ نمبر ۱۲۳۵۶) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ تفریق کرا دے (۲) اس دور میں شوہر کے ذمے قرض لینا مشکل ہے اور اسلامی حکومت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے عورت مجبور ہوتی ہے اس لئے حالات سنگین ہو تو تفریق کرادے لغت استدینی : قرض لے لیں۔
Book of Maintenance. 2146. And if a man is absent and he has property in the hand of a man who acknowledges it and acknowledges the marriage, then she takes a loan and lives. [Commentary: The harm of separation is greater than the harm of not paying maintenance, so separation is not done, and the loss of maintenance is compensated by taking a loan. So the woman suffers no great loss. (2) There is an athar from al-Hasan: 'If a man is unable to pay his wife's maintenance, separation is not made between them.' And al-Zuhri said: 'She is given respite.' He said: 'And it reached me that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz said that.' ... This athar shows that separation is not made; rather the woman takes a loan against the husband. (3) The hadith of the wife of Abu Sufyan can also be used as evidence... He said: 'Take what suffices you and your child in a reasonable manner.' Imam al-Shafi'i says that separation should be made. There is an athar: 'I asked Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib about a man who is unable to pay his wife's maintenance; he said: Separation is made between them. I said: Is it sunna? He said: Sunna.' ... This athar shows that separation is made. (2) In this era, taking a loan is difficult, and due to the lack of an Islamic government, the woman is constrained; therefore, if circumstances are severe, separation may be made. Lexicon: take a loan.]
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from Al Sharh Ul Sameri addresses the scenario where a husband is absent or unable to pay maintenance. The author begins by stating that if the husband is absent and has property with someone who acknowledges both the property and the marriage, the wife can take a loan in the husband's name. The reasoning provided is that the harm of separation (tafriq) is greater than the harm of non-payment, so separation is not the preferred solution; instead, the wife can borrow money to cover her needs, and the loan becomes the husband's debt. This is supported by the recorded statement (athar) of Hasan al-Basri who explicitly says that separation is not made when the husband cannot pay, and by al-Zuhri's view that she should be given time. Additionally, the hadith of Hind bint Utbah (wife of Abu Sufyan) is cited, where the Prophet ﷺ permitted her to take a reasonable amount to cover herself and her children when her husband was stingy. This hadith is used as evidence that the wife can take from the husband's property without his permission when he fails to provide. The passage then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that separation should be made. The Shafi'i position is supported by an athar from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, who states that separation is sunna. The commentary concludes with a practical observation: in later times (the era of the commentator), taking a loan is difficult and without an Islamic state to enforce it, the wife may be constrained; thus, in such circumstances, separation may be allowed. This reflects a nuanced Hanafi approach that considers the wife's hardship.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqa): Financial maintenance obligated upon the husband for his wife and children, including food, clothing, shelter.
قرض (qard): A loan taken by the wife from a third party with the intention that the husband becomes liable for repayment.
تفريق (tafriq): Judicial separation between spouses due to the husband's failure to fulfill obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi remedy for a wife whose husband is unable to pay maintenance?
A) Immediate judicial separation
B) Taking a loan in the husband's name
C) Filing a complaint for punishment
D) Moving out of the marital home

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage stability is prioritized: when a husband cannot pay maintenance, the wife takes a loan rather than seeking immediate separation, though later scholars permit separation in extreme hardship.

Maintenance from Absent Husband's Property: Scope and Guarantor
Ruling with proofs: judge's authority to allocate maintenance from absent husband's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories for whom maintenance may be allocated from an absent husband's property and explain why a guarantor is required.

Core Idea
The judge allocates maintenance for wife, minor children, and parents; a guarantor prevents misuse; other relatives are excluded.

This passage addresses the situation where an absent husband has property held by a third party who acknowledges both the property and the marriage. The judge is authorized to order maintenance from that property, but only for specific family members. A guarantor is required to safeguard the property. The rulings are supported by athar and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband is absent, his property is held by someone who acknowledges it and the marriage.
No need for the wife to prove marriage with witnesses; the acknowledgment suffices.
2
List the beneficiaries
The judge allocates maintenance for the wife, minor children, and parents of the absent husband.
Only these three categories are mentioned; other relatives are excluded.
3
Apply the guarantor requirement
The judge must take a guarantor from the wife to oversee proper use of the property and prevent misuse.
The guarantor ensures that the funds are not wasted or embezzled.
4
Recognize the limitation
The judge does not decree maintenance from the absent person's property for anyone other than these three categories.
This limitation is explicitly stated in ruling 21.
5
Note the proofs
The ruling is supported by an athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab, an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, and the hadith of Hind (ra).
These proofs establish the liability of the absent husband's property and the procedure.

[۲۱۴۸] (۲۱) اور نہ فیصلہ کرے غائب کے مال میں نفقة مگر ان لوگوں کے لئے۔
2146 (19) If a man is absent and he has property in the hand of a man who acknowledges it and acknowledges the marriage, then she takes a loan. The judge (allocates) from that property the maintenance of the absent one's wife, his small children, and his parents. [2147] (20) And he takes a guarantor from her for it. (21) And he does not decree maintenance from the property of the absent one except for these. There is an athar from Ibn Umar that Umar ibn al-Khattab wrote to the commanders of troops concerning men absent from their wives, commanding them to force them to pay or divorce, and if they divorced, they would send maintenance for the period they were held. ... This shows that the absent man's property is liable for his wife's maintenance. And regarding taking a loan, there is an athar from Ibrahim: 'If she takes a loan, it is upon him; and what she consumes from her own property is not upon him.' ... This shows that if she takes a loan by the judge's instruction, the maintenance is from the husband; otherwise, it is from her own property. And if the husband does not pay adequate maintenance, the woman has the right to take adequate maintenance for herself and her children. For this, the hadith of Aisha related by Hind... 'Take what suffices you and your child in a reasonable manner.' ... This hadith shows that if the husband has property but does not pay adequate maintenance, the woman may take adequate maintenance for herself and her children from his property. 2147 (20) And the judge takes a guarantor from the woman. That is, when the judge orders the woman to take maintenance from the absent husband's property, he designates a guarantor to oversee the property to prevent excessive spending and embezzlement. ... There is an athar that Ibn Abi Layla used to send women to check her pregnancy; if they confirmed, he gave her maintenance and took a guarantor from her. [2148] (21) And he does not decree maintenance from the absent one's property except for these people.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage continues the previous discussion but focuses on the scenario where the absent husband actually has property held by someone who acknowledges both the property and the marriage. The first ruling (19) states that the judge is to allocate maintenance from that property for the absent husband's wife, his minor children, and his parents. The author explains that once the acknowledgment is made, there is no need for the wife to prove her marriage by witnesses; the judge can directly order payment. This is supported by an athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab, who wrote to troop commanders concerning absent husbands, directing them to compel either payment of maintenance or divorce. If the husband chose divorce, he still had to pay maintenance for the time he was absent. This shows that the absent husband's property is liable for maintenance. An additional athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i clarifies that if the wife takes a loan with the judge's authorization, it becomes the husband's debt; but if she spends from her own property, it is not recoverable from him. The second ruling (20) mandates that the judge must take a guarantor (kafil) from the wife. This is to ensure that the property taken is not misused or overspent. The guarantor oversees the expenditure to give peace of mind to both parties. This is supported by an athar describing the practice of Ibn Abi Layla. The third ruling (21) limits the judge's authority: he may only decree maintenance from the absent person's property for the specific individuals mentioned—the wife, minor children, and parents—not for other relatives. This passage also reiterates the hadith of Hind, affirming that if the husband is present but fails to pay adequate maintenance, the wife may take what is sufficient from his property without permission.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): A guarantor or surety appointed by the judge from the wife to ensure proper use of the husband's property taken for maintenance.
غائب (gha'ib): A husband who is absent from his home, whether for travel, imprisonment, or other reasons, and unable to personally provide maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Any needy relative

Key Takeaway
The judge's authority to order maintenance from an absent husband's property is limited to wife, minor children, and parents, and requires a guarantor.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Maintenance from Absent Husband's Wealth: Scope and Proofs
Ruling with proofs on maintenance from absent husband's wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on maintenance from an absent husband's wealth, identify the three categories of recipients, and explain the legal reasoning (qada' al-gha'ib) that limits the judge's authority.

Core Idea
A judge can order maintenance from an absent husband's wealth only for wife, young children, and parents.

When a husband is absent and fails to provide maintenance, his dependents may seek a judge's order to access his property. However, Hanafi law imposes strict limits: the judge can only authorize maintenance for the wife, minor children, and parents. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proofs from the Qur'an, Sunnah, and athar, and the procedural principle of qada' al-gha'ib that prevents orders for other relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Carefully read the initial statement: 'The judge orders from that wealth the maintenance of the absent husband's wife, his young children, and his parents. And he does not order maintenance from the wealth of the absent except for these.' This is the core ruling.
Note the three categories: wife, young children, parents.
2
Examine the proofs (athar and hadith)
The passage cites several proofs: the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab (compelling absent husbands), the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i (loan liability), the hadith of Hind bint Utbah (taking without knowledge), and the practice of Ibn Abi Laila (taking a guarantor). For each, identify how it supports the ruling.
Focus on the hadith of Hind, which is the core proof for a woman's right to take maintenance.
3
Understand the principle of qada' al-gha'ib
The passage explains that judgment against an absent person (qada' al-gha'ib) is generally not allowed because the defendant must be present to defend, as proven by the hadiths of Ali and Ibn al-Zubayr. This is why the judge cannot order maintenance for relatives beyond the three categories.
Exception: when the obligation is already established by Shariah, the judge's order is merely confirmatory.
4
Note the exception for confirmed obligations
The passage states that maintenance for these three categories is already wajib by Shariah. Therefore, the judge's order is not establishing a new obligation but confirming an existing one. This is why it is allowed despite the general prohibition on qada' al-gha'ib.
This exception is specific to these three categories; for others, the judge cannot order.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a case where an absent husband has left behind wealth. His wife requests maintenance for herself, their minor children, his elderly parents, and his unemployed adult brother. According to this ruling, which can be ordered? Only the first three. Explain why the brother is excluded.
The brother's maintenance is not established as wajib upon the husband by Shariah, so the judge cannot order it without the husband being present.

ان تأخذ بغير علمه ما يكفيها وولدها بالمعروف ص ۸۰۸ نمبر ۵۳۶۴) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ شوہر کے پاس مال ہو اور وہ مناسب نفقہ نہ دیتا ہو تو عورت اپنے لئے اور اپنی اولاد کے لئے مناسب نفقہ شوہر کے مال سے دونوں قاضی کے سامنے بیٹھے اور یہ اسی صورت میں ہو سکتا ہے جب دونوں حاضر ہوں ۔ اس لئے قضاء علی الغائب جائز نہیں ہے۔
The judge [orders] from that wealth the maintenance of the absent husband's wife, his young children, and his parents. And he takes from her a guarantor for it. And he does not order maintenance from the wealth of the absent except for these. There is an athar from Ibn Umar that Umar ibn al-Khattab wrote to the commanders of the armies regarding men who were absent from their wives, ordering them to take hold of them so that they either spend or divorce. If they divorce, they should send the maintenance for the period they detained. (Reference) From this it is known that the wife's maintenance is from the wealth of the absent husband. And regarding taking a loan, there is this athar: from Ibrahim, he said: If she takes a loan, then it is upon him, but if she eats from her own wealth, then it is not upon him. (Reference) From this athar it is known that if she takes a loan by the judge's order, then it will be from the husband's wealth, otherwise it will be from her own wealth. And if the husband does not pay appropriate maintenance, the woman has the right to take appropriate maintenance for herself and her children from his wealth. For this, there is this hadith: from Aisha that Hind bint Utbah said: O Messenger of Allah, Abu Sufyan is a miserly man and does not give me enough for myself and my children except what I take from him without his knowledge. He said: Take what suffices you and your children in a reasonable manner. (Reference) From this hadith it is known that if the husband has wealth and does not give appropriate maintenance, the woman can take appropriate maintenance for herself and her children from his wealth. There is an athar: Ibn Abi Laila used to send women to her to look at her, i.e., to the pregnancy, and if they recognized that and she confirmed it, he would give her the maintenance and take a guarantor from her. (Reference) [These are] the ones for whom [the judge orders]. For a man who is absent, he may order maintenance from his wealth for the wife, young children, and parents. He should not order maintenance for others. The maintenance of these people is obligatory by Shariah even before the order; there is no need for an order. However, by ordering, it becomes confirmed. So the order is for confirmation, not for establishment. Therefore, he can order maintenance for these. Another reason: judgment cannot be passed on an absent person. Therefore, one cannot order maintenance from the wealth of the absent for others besides the wife, young children, and parents. Regarding not judging on the absent, there is this hadith: From Ali, he said: The Messenger of Allah sent me to Yemen as a judge... When the two litigants sit before you, do not judge until you hear from the other as you heard from the first, for it is more appropriate that the judgment becomes clear to you. (Reference) From this hadith it is known that one should hear the other before judging, and this is only possible when the defendant is present. Therefore, judging on the absent is not permissible. Another hadith: From Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr, he said: The Messenger of Allah ruled that the two litigants should sit before the judge. (Reference) This hadith states that both sit before the judge, and this is only possible when both are present. Therefore, judgment on the absent is not permissible.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs. It establishes that the judge can order maintenance from the absent husband's wealth only for his wife, young children, and parents. The judge also takes a guarantor from the wife to ensure proper use. The proofs include: (1) The practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab who wrote to army commanders to compel absent husbands to either provide maintenance or divorce, and if they divorced, they had to pay maintenance for the past period. (2) The statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that if the wife takes a loan by the judge's order, the husband is liable; otherwise, she must use her own wealth. (3) The hadith of Hind bint Utbah, where the Prophet permitted her to take sufficient maintenance from her husband Abu Sufyan without his knowledge when he was miserly. (4) The practice of Ibn Abi Laila who took a guarantor from a pregnant woman when granting maintenance. The commentary then explains why the judge cannot order maintenance for other relatives: the maintenance of these three categories is already obligatory by Shariah, so the judge's order is merely confirmatory. However, for others, judging on an absent person is not allowed because the defendant must be present to defend himself, as proven by the hadiths of Ali and Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr. This is a Hanafi position: the judge cannot pass a ruling against an absent person except in these specific cases where the obligation is already established by Shariah.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance: food, clothing, and lodging that a husband must provide for his wife and certain relatives.
غائب (gha'ib): A person who is absent from his residence, such that his wife and dependents are left without maintenance.
كفيل (kafil): A guarantor who ensures that the maintenance taken from the absent husband's wealth is used properly.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment against an absent person; generally not allowed in Hanafi law except in a few cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, from whose wealth can the judge order maintenance for the wife of an absent husband?
A) Only from the wife's wealth
B) From the absent husband's wealth
C) From the judge's own wealth
D) From the community fund

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the judge may order maintenance from an absent husband's wealth only for his wife, young children, and parents, because their maintenance is already wajib by Shariah, thus avoiding the prohibition of judgment against an absent person.

Adjusting Maintenance When Husband's Status Changes
Ruling with proofs on adjusting maintenance when husband's financial status changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that maintenance must be adjusted when the husband's financial status changes from poverty to wealth, and cite the supporting hadith and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
A maintenance order is not fixed; it changes with the husband's financial ability.

In Hanafi law, maintenance (nafaqah) is determined by the husband's current financial ability. If a judge initially orders a lower amount due to poverty and the husband later becomes wealthy, the wife can seek an increase. This lesson examines the proofs from hadith and Quran that support this dynamic ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial maintenance order
Understand that the judge ordered maintenance based on the husband's poverty (i'sar). This was a valid order given the circumstances.
The order is not final; it reflects the husband's state at that time.
2
Recognize the change in status
The husband becomes wealthy (aysara). The wife sues to have the maintenance increased to match his new wealth.
The wife must prove the change in court.
3
Apply the hadith evidence
The hadith 'Feed them what you eat and clothe them what you wear' (Sunan Abu Dawud) establishes that maintenance should match the husband's own standard of living.
The imperative 'اطعمون' indicates obligation; the standard is the husband's own consumption.
4
Apply the Quranic evidence
The verse 'Let the man of abundance spend out of his abundance' (Surah al-Talaq 65:7) directly commands a wealthy man to provide generously.
Combined with the hadith, it forms a clear proof for adjusting maintenance upward.

[Commentary: If the judge ordered maintenance from the wealth of an absent man for his wife, young children, and parents, he should not order for others. The maintenance of these people is obligatory by Shariah even before the order; there is no need for an order. However, by ordering, it becomes confirmed. So the order is for confirmation, not for establishment. Therefore, he can order maintenance for these. Another reason: judgment cannot be passed on an absent person. Therefore, one cannot order maintenance from the wealth of the absent for others besides the wife, young children, and parents. Regarding not judging on the absent, there is this hadith: From Ali, he said: The Messenger of Allah sent me to Yemen as a judge... When the two litigants sit before you, do not judge until you hear from the other as you heard from the first, for it is more appropriate that the judgment becomes clear to you. (Reference) From this hadith it is known that one should hear the other before judging, and this is only possible when the defendant is present. Therefore, judging on the absent is not permissible. Another hadith: From Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr, he said: The Messenger of Allah ruled that the two litigants should sit before the judge. (Reference) This hadith states that both sit before the judge, and this is only possible when both are present. Therefore, judgment on the absent is not permissible. [2149] (22) If the judge ordered maintenance for her due to poverty, then [the husband] becomes wealthy, and she sues him, he completes it for her with the maintenance of wealth. The husband was previously poor, so the judge ordered maintenance according to poverty. Later he became wealthy, and the wife filed a case with the judge claiming he is wealthy and proved it, then the judge now orders maintenance according to wealth. The maintenance of poverty was due to the compulsion of poverty; now that he has become wealthy, the maintenance of wealth becomes obligatory. (Reference) In hadith: The man should give maintenance according to his capacity. So when he becomes wealthy, he gives the maintenance of the wealthy. The verse is: Let the man of abundance spend out of his abundance. (Surah al-Talaq 65:7) This verse also indicates the judgment of wealth. A wealthy man must give maintenance of the wealthy.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs on adjusting maintenance when the husband's financial condition changes from poverty to wealth. The text first restates the earlier ruling about judging on the absent (wife, young children, parents) and then presents a new ruling: if the judge initially ordered maintenance based on the husband's poverty, and later the husband becomes wealthy, the wife can sue and the judge must increase the maintenance to the level of the wealthy. The proof includes the hadith 'Feed them what you eat and clothe them what you wear' (Sunan Abu Dawud), which indicates that maintenance should match the husband's own standard of living. Also, the Quranic verse 'Let the man of abundance spend out of his abundance' (Surah al-Talaq 65:7) supports that a wealthy man must provide generous maintenance. The Hanafi position is clear: maintenance is determined by the husband's current ability, not a fixed amount, so a change in financial status justifies a change in the maintenance order.

Key Terms
اعسار (i'sar): Poverty or financial hardship; the state of being unable to afford maintenance.
ايسار (aysara): To become wealthy or financially able after a period of poverty.

Nafaqah from Absent Husband and Adjustment Upon Wealth Change
Ruling on nafaqah from absent husband's wealth and subsequent increase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah from an absent husband's wealth, explain why the judge's decision is limited to specific relatives, and apply the rule that nafaqah adjusts when the husband's financial status changes.

Core Idea
A judge may only award nafaqah from an absent husband's wealth for wife, young children, and parents, and must increase it if the husband becomes wealthy.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Thamiri addresses two connected issues: which relatives can receive nafaqah from an absent husband's wealth, and whether a previous nafaqah ruling can be increased if the husband becomes wealthy. The author explains the Hanafi position that only three categories are eligible because judging on an absent is generally not allowed, and the obligation for others is not established. He also provides proofs from hadith and Quran for adjusting nafaqah according to the husband's means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
What can a judge do regarding an absent husband's nafaqah? When can the wife demand an increase? Extract the two distinct rulings from the passage.
The first ruling limits eligible recipients; the second addresses changes in financial status.
2
Understand the limitation on absent-party judgments
Why are only wife, young children, and parents eligible? What procedural principle prevents judging on an absent person? Locate the hadith and reasoning.
The principle is that qada al-ghaib (judging on an absent) is disallowed unless the obligation is already established by Shariah.
3
Examine the proofs for each ruling
For the first ruling: how do the hadith of Ali and Ibn Zubayr support the limitation? For the second: how do the hadith of Muawiyah and Quran 65:7 prove adjustment?
Focus on wajh al-dalalah – how each proof leads to the ruling.
4
Apply the rulings to a scenario
Given a scenario of an absent husband with wealth, determine which relatives qualify for nafaqah and whether a prior ruling can be increased if the husband becomes wealthy.
Use the shurut (conditions) and arkan (categories) identified.

Kitab al-Nafaqat: For these [people][?]. (22) And if the judge ruled for her nafaqah of the poor, then he became wealthy, and she litigated, then he completes for her [the nafaqah of the wealthy]. [Urdu]: For an absent man, the judge may decide nafaqah for his wife, young children, and parents from his wealth. He may not decide for others. Their nafaqah is obligatory by Shariah before the decision; the decision is only a confirmation. The reason is that one cannot judge against an absent person. Evidence: Hadith of Ali: 'Do not judge until you hear from the other side.' Also hadith of Ibn Zubayr: 'Both litigants sit before the judge.' Therefore judging on an absent is not allowed. (22) If the judge ruled for her nafaqah due to poverty, then the husband became wealthy, she claims; the judge should complete for her the nafaqah of the wealthy. Because the husband must provide from his own means. Evidence: Hadith of Muawiyah: 'Feed them from what you eat and clothe them from what you wear.' Also Quran: 'Let the wealthy spend from his wealth.' (Surah al-Talaq 65:7).
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning nafaqah from an absent husband's wealth and the adjustment of nafaqah when his financial status changes. The text begins by stating that a judge may only award nafaqah from the wealth of an absent husband for three categories: his wife, his young children, and his parents. This is because the obligation of nafaqah for these relatives is established by Shariah before any judicial decision; the judge's ruling merely confirms it. The limitation is due to the principle that one cannot pass a judgment against an absent person, as supported by two hadith: the first from Ali (radi Allahu anhu) where the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wasallam) instructed him to hear both sides before judging, and the second from Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr (radi Allahu anhu) that both litigants must be present. Therefore, any nafaqah beyond these categories cannot be enforced in the absence of the husband. The second part of the passage addresses a scenario where the judge initially ruled for nafaqah based on the husband's poverty, but later the husband becomes wealthy. In this case, the wife can claim an increase to the nafaqah corresponding to the husband's new wealth. This is supported by the hadith of Muawiyah al-Qushayri and the Quranic verse 'Let the wealthy spend from his wealth' (Surah al-Talaq 65:7). The ruling emphasizes that nafaqah is based on the husband's current ability, and a change in his financial status requires a corresponding adjustment.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance or financial support that a husband must provide for his wife and certain relatives.
اعسار (i'sar): Poverty or financial hardship; the state of being unable to afford expenses.
موسر (musir): Wealthy person; one who has the means to provide a higher level of nafaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from the wealth of an absent husband, for which relatives can a judge award nafaqah?
A) Wife, young children, and parents only
B) Wife and children of any age
C) All relatives including siblings
D) Only the wife

Key Takeaway
A judge may only award nafaqah from an absent husband's wealth to his wife, young children, and parents, and if the husband's financial status improves, the nafaqah must be increased accordingly.

When Past Nafaqah Becomes Enforceable: Prior Order or Settlement
Ruling on claiming past nafaqah without prior order or settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on past nafaqah and identify the two conditions that make it enforceable.

Core Idea
Past nafaqah is not a debt; it becomes owed only after a judicial order or mutual settlement.

This passage addresses a practical question: if a husband fails to provide nafaqah for some time, can the wife later claim those arrears? The Hanafi school answers based on the nature of nafaqah as a form of kinship support (silah rahmi) rather than a wage or debt. The ruling clarifies that without a prior judge's order (qada') or a settlement (sulh) with the husband, past nafaqah is not legally enforceable. The proof is an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is claiming past nafaqah (nafaqah of previous months). The ruling is that the wife has no claim unless one of two conditions is met: a prior judicial order fixing the amount, or a settlement between husband and wife.
This is a ruling about enforceability, not about the husband's moral obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'If she borrowed, it is taken from him until they separate; if she did not borrow, she has nothing against him if she spent from her own wealth.' This is found in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
The athar is a saying of a tabi'i, not a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ). Its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar distinguishes between a wife who borrows for expenses and one who uses her own wealth. The borrowed amount is recoverable from the husband, but if she used her own money without borrowing, she has no claim. This implies that past nafaqah does not automatically become a debt—only if she took a loan (which can be traced back to the husband's obligation) can she recover.
The key phrase is 'if she did not borrow, she has nothing against him'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The commentary mentions that 'others say from the day she takes her case to the authorities'—meaning that some scholars (likely non-Hanafi) hold that nafaqah becomes due from the date of complaint. The Hanafi position requires either a prior order or settlement.
This is a cross-madhhab difference.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that past nafaqah is not enforceable without prior qada' or sulh. The reasoning is that nafaqah is silah rahmi, not a wage; it does not create a debt automatically. The athar supports this. The opposite view is weaker in Hanafi usul.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin, meaning this is the relied-upon position.

Source Text

Nafaqah of the wealthy. (23) And if a period passed during which the husband did not spend on her, and she claimed it from him, then there is nothing for her unless the judge had fixed a nafaqah for her or she settled with her husband on an amount, then the judge would rule for her the nafaqah of the past. [Urdu]: If a wife did not receive nafaqah for some months and now wants it, the ruling is that she can only collect it if the judge had previously ordered it or if the husband had settled an amount. Otherwise, she cannot legally claim it. This is because nafaqah is considered sila rahmi (tie of kinship), not a wage. Therefore, without a prior order or settlement, there is no obligation. An athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'If she borrowed, it is taken from him until they separate; if she did not borrow, she has nothing against him if she spent from her own wealth.' Others say from the day she takes her case to the authorities.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage rules on the situation where a husband fails to provide nafaqah for some time, and the wife later seeks those arrears. The Hanafi position is that she cannot claim past nafaqah unless there has been a judicial order fixing the amount or a private settlement with the husband. The reasoning is that nafaqah is not a debt but a form of kinship support (silah rahmi); it does not become an enforceable liability without prior specification. The text cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i to support this: if the wife had to borrow for her expenses, that loan can be recovered from the husband, but if she simply used her own wealth, she cannot claim back. The commentary notes that some scholars hold that nafaqah becomes due from the day she reports the matter to the judge. However, the Hanafi mufta bihi is that only a prior order or settlement creates an enforceable right to past nafaqah.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling or order; specifically, a judge's decision fixing the amount of nafaqah.
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise between husband and wife regarding the amount of past nafaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She can always claim it for any past period
B) Only if the judge previously fixed the nafaqah or the husband settled with her
C) Only if she borrowed money for her expenses
D) If she lived separately from her husband

Key Takeaway
Past nafaqah is not automatically a debt; it becomes enforceable only through a prior judicial order (qada') or settlement (sulh).

Nafaqah Obligation Ceases with Husband's Death
Ruling that nafaqah arrears are nullified upon husband's death before payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on unpaid nafaqah arrears after the husband's death and explain why it does not become a debt on the estate.

Core Idea
Unpaid nafaqah is cancelled at death because it is a gift, not a debt, and requires possession.

A court order for nafaqah does not turn the obligation into a debt that survives the husband. In the Hanafi school, if the husband dies before paying, the arrears are nullified. This seemingly harsh rule is based on the nature of nafaqah as a form of support (silah) that only becomes a property right upon actual possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State clearly: if the husband dies after a nafaqah order but before payment, the unpaid nafaqah is saqit (cancelled).
The wife cannot recover it from the husband's estate.
2
Understand the underlying reason
Nafaqah is classified as silah (a gift/kinship support), not a debt. For a silah, possession (qabd) is required for the obligation to become a property right. Without possession, the obligation lapses at death.
Contrast with a loan, which becomes a debt.
3
Examine the dalil
Analyze the athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: if the wife borrowed, it is recoverable; if she used her own wealth, she has no claim. This supports the principle that only a pre-existing debt (through borrowing) survives.
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
4
Compare with the opposing view
Some schools (e.g., Malikis) consider nafaqah as a debt that transmits to the estate. Recognize the difference and the Hanafi response.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text

[۲۱۵۱] (۲۴) اگر نفقے کے فیصلے کے بعد شوہر مر گیا اور کچھ مہینے گزر گئے تو نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
قاضی نے نفقے کا فیصلہ کیا اس کے بعد شوہر تین ماہ تک زندہ رہا لیکن اس مدت کا نفقہ ادا نہیں کیا اور شوہر مر گیا تو ان تین مہینوں کا نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ شوہر کے چھوڑے ہوئے مال سے وصول نہیں کر سکے گی۔ نفقہ صلہ ہے اور صلہ پر قبضہ نہ کرے تو وہ اس کا نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اور اب شوہر بھی حیات نہیں رہا کہ اس سے وصول کر سکے اس لئے ساقط ہو جائے گا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن النخعی فال اذا ادانت اخذ به حتى يقضى عنها وان لم تستدن فلا شيء لها عليه اذا اكلت من مالها (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل يغيب عن امرأته فلا شفق علیهاج سابع ص ۹۴ نمبر ۱۲۳۴۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ شوہر کے نام قرض لے گی تب شوہر کے ذمے ہو گا اور اپنا مال خرچ کیا تو شوہر سے وصول نہیں کر سکے گی۔ اسی طرح وصول کرنے سے پہلے حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت مخفی فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر بیوی نے قرض لیا تو شوہر سے لیا جائے گا جب تک کہ الگ ہونے کا فیصلہ نہ کر دیا جائے۔ اور اگر قرض نہیں تو عورت کا شوہر پر کچھ نہیں ہے اگر عورت نے اپنا مال کھایا۔ حضرت معمر فر ماتے ہیں کہ دوسرے حضرات یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جس دن سے معاملہ بادشاہ کے پاس لے گئی اس دن سے نفقہ ملے گا (ب) حضرت شخصی فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر قرض لیا تو شوہر سے لیا جائے گا جب تک کہ الگ ہونے کا فیصلہ نہ کر دیا جائے اور اگر قرض نہیں لیا تو عورت کا شوہر پر کچھ نہیں ہے اگر عورت نے اپنا مال کھایا۔
(24) If the husband died after the judge ruled for nafaqah and months passed [without payment], then the nafaqah is nullified. [Urdu]: After the judge's order, if the husband lived for three months without paying and then died, the nafaqah for those months is cancelled. She cannot recover it from his estate because nafaqah is a gift (silah) and unless she takes possession, it does not become her property. An athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'If she borrowed, it is taken from him; if she did not borrow, she has nothing.'
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage deals with the fate of nafaqah arrears when the husband dies before paying them. The ruling is that the nafaqah is cancelled (saqit) and cannot be claimed from the husband's estate. The reason given is that nafaqah is considered a form of gift or kinship support (silah), not a debt. For it to become a property right, the wife must have taken physical possession (qabd) of the amount or its equivalent. Since the payment was not made before the husband's death, the obligation lapses. The text supports this with an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, which states that if the wife had borrowed money for her expenses, that loan can be recovered from the husband, but if she simply used her own wealth, she has no claim. The commentary also notes that some scholars argue that nafaqah becomes a debt from the day the wife takes her case to the judge, but the Hanafi mufta bihi is that without actual receipt or a prior settlement, the obligation ceases at death.

Key Terms
ساقط (saqit): Legally null and void; the nafaqah obligation is extinguished.
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking actual receipt of the nafaqah funds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It becomes a debt on the husband's estate that must be paid
B) It is cancelled and cannot be claimed from the estate
C) The wife can claim it from the husband's inheritors
D) It is considered a gift that automatically transfers to the wife

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, unpaid nafaqah arrears are cancelled upon the husband's death because nafaqah is a gift requiring possession, not a debt.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

When Can a Wife Claim Past Maintenance?
Ruling on past maintenance with proof from athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on past maintenance and identify the two conditions that make it enforceable.

Core Idea
Without a qadi's decree or settlement, past maintenance is not owed — it is a gift, not a wage.

This passage addresses a common question: can a wife demand maintenance for past months when the husband failed to provide? The Hanafi school holds a distinctive position — maintenance is not automatically a debt for the past. Instead, it becomes enforceable only if the judge had previously fixed it or if the couple had agreed on a settlement. This ruling is rooted in the understanding of maintenance as an act of kindness (silah rahmi) rather than a wage (ujrah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The passage states: if a period passes without the husband spending, and the wife later demands it, nothing is due to her. This is the default ruling for past maintenance.
Applies only to past periods; ongoing maintenance remains wajib.
2
Spot the two exceptions
The ruling changes if either (1) the qadi had previously fixed a maintenance amount for that past period, or (2) the wife and husband reached a settlement on the amount. In either case, judgment is given for the past maintenance.
The qadi's decree or settlement makes the past maintenance enforceable.
3
Understand the legal nature of maintenance
The commentator clarifies that in Hanafi fiqh, maintenance is not a wage (ujrah) but a gift (silah). Therefore, it does not automatically become a debt for past periods without a specific legal act (qadi's decree or settlement).
Compare with other schools that may treat it as a debt.
4
Analyze the proof (athar)
The author cites Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: if the wife borrows money to cover her needs, that borrowing creates a claim against the husband; if she does not borrow, she has no claim. This supports the principle that past maintenance is not automatically owed.
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, showing early Hanafi reliance on the practice of the salaf.

Source Text

۲۱۵۰) (۲۳) اگر گزرگئی کچھ مدت اور شوہر نے اس پر خرچ نہیں کیا اور عورت نے اس کا مطالبہ کیا تو اس کے لئے کچھ نہیں ہوگا مگر یہ کہ قاضی نے اس کے لئے نفقہ مقرر کیا ہو یا شوہر سے کسی مقدار پر صلح کرلی ہو تو فیصلہ ہوگا اس کے لئے گزشتہ نفقہ کا۔

Chapter of Maintenance. Maintenance of the wealthy. (23) And if a period passes and the husband did not spend on her and she demands that, then nothing is due to her unless the judge fixed maintenance for her or she settled with the husband on an amount, then judgment is given for her for the past maintenance. (Urdu commentary: If some time passed and the husband did not spend, and the woman demands, then nothing for her unless the judge fixed or she settled...) There is a tradition: from al-Nakha'i, he said: If she borrows, it is taken from him until he is separated from her, and if she does not borrow, then nothing is due to her from him if she eats from her own wealth. Ma'mar said: And others say from the day she raises her matter to the ruler. This tradition indicates that from the time she takes the matter to the ruler, the woman becomes entitled to maintenance.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on past maintenance (nafaqah madhiyah). The author states that if a husband has not spent on his wife for some time and she later demands it, she cannot claim anything unless the qadi had previously fixed maintenance or she had a settlement with the husband. The commentary explains that in the Hanafi school, maintenance is considered a gift (silah) not a wage, so it is not automatically owed for past periods. The only exception is if the qadi decreed it or the husband agreed. The proof cited is an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: if the wife borrows money for her needs, it becomes a debt on the husband until he is separated from her; otherwise, she gets nothing if she used her own wealth. Ma'mar reports that others say maintenance is due from the day she takes the matter to the ruler. The Hanafi position, following Nakha'i, is that without a decree or settlement, past maintenance is not enforceable.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Spousal maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter.
صلہ رحمی (silah rahmi): Kindness to kin; here used to characterize maintenance as a gift, not a contractual obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, what is the key condition for a wife's claim for past maintenance?
A) She must have borrowed money.
B) The husband must be wealthy.
C) The qadi must have fixed it or she must have settled with the husband.
D) She must have raised the matter to the ruler.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, past maintenance is not automatically owed; it becomes enforceable only through a qadi's decree or a settlement, reflecting the view that maintenance is a gift, not a wage.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
6 min
Maintenance Lapses After Husband's Death
Hanafi ruling on maintenance after husband dies before paying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on unpaid maintenance after the husband's death and explain the legal reasoning using the gift analogy and the supporting athar.

Core Idea
Unpaid maintenance is not a debt; it lapses on death because it is a gift not possessed.

What happens when a husband dies without paying court-ordered maintenance? The Hanafi school holds that the maintenance lapses and cannot be collected from the estate. This may seem harsh, but it is based on a specific legal construct: nafaqah is treated as a gift (silah), which requires physical possession. We will examine the ruling and the evidence from al-Nakha'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the legal question: what happens to court-ordered maintenance (nafaqah) if the husband dies before paying? The ruling is that the maintenance lapses (suqut).
The ruling applies even if the qadi had decreed the amount.
2
Understand the legal construction
In Hanafi fiqh, maintenance is considered a gift (silah) rather than a debt. A gift only becomes owned after physical possession (qabd). Since the wife did not receive the payment before the husband's death, it never became her property.
This construction distinguishes maintenance from debts, which survive death.
3
Examine the proof (athar)
The author cites an athar from al-Nakha'i: if the wife borrows in the husband's name, it becomes a debt; otherwise, she cannot claim anything. This supports the idea that maintenance does not automatically become a claimable debt.
The athar is zanni al-thubut but provides a key precedent.
4
Apply the proof to the case
Connecting the athar to the ruling: without borrowing, unpaid maintenance lapses upon death. The wife cannot recover from the husband's estate.
The ruling is mufta bihi (operative) in the Hanafi school.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools may treat unpaid maintenance as a debt on the estate. The Hanafi position rests on the specific classification of maintenance as a gift.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; each school has its own evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۵۱] (۲۴) فان مات الزوج بعد ما قضى عليه بالنفقة ومضت شهور الاعسار : تنگدست، الموسر : مالدار

۲۱۵۱) (۲۴) اگر نفقے کے فیصلے کے بعد شوہر مر گیا اور کچھ مہینے گزر گئے تو نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
قاضی نے نفقے کا فیصلہ کیا اس کے بعد شوہر تین ماہ تک زندہ رہا لیکن اس مدت کا نفقہ ادا نہیں کیا اور شوہر مر گیا تو ان تین مہینوں کا نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ شوہر کے چھوڑے ہوئے مال سے وصول نہیں کر سکے گی۔ نفقہ صلہ ہے اور صلہ پر قبضہ نہ کرے تو وہ اس کا نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اور اب شوہر بھی حیات نہیں رہا کہ اس سے وصول کر سکے اس لئے ساقط ہو جائے گا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن النخعی فال اذا ادانت اخذ به حتى يقضى عنها وان لم تستدن فلا شيء لها عليه اذا اكلت من مالها (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل يغيب عن امرأته فلا شفق علیہاج سابع ص ۹۴ نمبر ۱۲۳۴۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ شوہر کے نام قرض لے گی تب شوہر کے ذمے ہو گا اور اپنا مال خرچ کیا تو شوہر سے وصول نہیں کر سکے گی۔ اسی طرح وصول کرنے سے پہلے شوہر کا انتقال ہو گا تو وہ نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
(24) And if the husband dies after judgment was passed against him for maintenance, and months pass, the maintenance lapses. (Urdu: If after the decree the husband dies and some months pass, the maintenance is dropped. Even if the qadi decreed, and the husband lived three months without paying, then dies, the maintenance for that period is forfeit; she cannot collect from his estate. Maintenance is a gift, and a gift not taken possession of does not become owned. The husband is no longer alive to collect from, so it lapses. And there is a tradition: from al-Nakha'i: if she borrows, it is taken from him until he is separated; if she does not borrow, nothing is due... This tradition shows that if she borrows in the husband's name, it becomes his debt; if she spent her own money, she cannot collect. Similarly, if before collecting the husband dies, the maintenance is forfeited.)
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This lesson addresses what happens to court-ordered maintenance if the husband dies before paying it. The ruling states that the maintenance lapses and cannot be recovered from the husband's estate. The reason given is that maintenance (nafaqah) is treated as a gift (silah) in Hanafi fiqh; a gift must be physically taken possession to become owned. Since the wife did not receive the payment before the husband's death, it never became her property. Therefore it does not remain as a debt against the estate. The author cites the same athar from al-Nakha'i to support the principle that without borrowing or taking possession, no claim exists. The Urdu commentary explains that even if the qadi had decreed the amount, if the husband died before payment, the right is extinguished.

Key Terms
سقوط (suqut): Lapse or forfeiture of a right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to court-ordered maintenance if the husband dies before paying it?
A) It becomes a debt payable from his estate.
B) It lapses and is not recoverable.
C) It is transferred to his heirs.
D) It must be paid by the wife's guardian.

Key Takeaway
Court-ordered maintenance not paid before the husband's death lapses and cannot be recovered from his estate.

Advance Maintenance and Death of Husband: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Hanafi ruling on advance maintenance and husband's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on advance maintenance and explain why Imam Muhammad differed, identifying the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Advance maintenance is a gift, not a wage — once given, it cannot be recovered, even if the husband dies mid-year.

When a husband gives his wife a year's worth of maintenance in advance and then dies before the year ends, should the wife refund the unused portion? The Hanafi school's majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say no: it is a gift, and ownership transfers upon delivery. But Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, viewing maintenance as compensation for the wife's marital confinement. This lesson dissects the dispute and its practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
Determine the scenario: husband advances a year's maintenance, then dies during that year. What happens to the remaining maintenance?
The dispute is about the nature of nafaqah: gift (hibah) vs. compensation (ihtibas).
2
State the Majority Hanafi Position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no refund. Maintenance is a gift, so once given, the wife owns it absolutely.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Understand the Majority's Proof
The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave annual maintenance to his wives and did not recover any upon his death.
The hadith from Bukhari is cited as evidence of the practice.
4
Understand Imam Muhammad's Position
Imam Muhammad argues that maintenance is compensation for 'ihtibas' (the wife's being bound to the marriage), like wages. Therefore, the wife deserves only for the days she lived with the husband; the rest is a debt to the estate.
This is a minority opinion within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf and Mufta Bihi
Practical difference: if husband dies after six months, wife keeps all (majority) vs. returns half (Imam Muhammad). The relied-upon opinion is the majority view (no refund).
The author of the text presents the majority view as primary and notes Imam Muhammad's disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۵۱] (۲۵) وان اسلفها نفقة سنة ثم مات لم يسترجع منها بشيء وقال محمد رحمه الله يُحتسب لها نفقة ما مضى وما بقى للزوج

(۲۵) اگر پیشگی دیدے ایک سال کا نفقہ پھر شوہر مر جائے تو اس سے کچھ واپس نہیں لے گا ۔ اور فرمایا امام محمد نے اس کے نطقے کا حساب کیا جائے گا جو گزر گیا اور جو شوہر کے لئے باقی رہا۔ مثلا شوہر نے ایک سال کا نفقہ بیوی کو دے دیا پھر چھ ماہ میں شوہر کا انتقال ہو گیا تو باقہ چھ ماہ کا نفقہ واپس نہیں لے گا ، وہ بیوی کے پاس
ہی رہے گا۔
بیوی کا نفقہ صلہ ہے اور ہدیہ ہے۔ اور ہدیہ دینے کے بعد وہ اس کا مالک ہو جاتا ہے اس لئے واپس نہیں لے گا (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ آپ سال بھر کا نفقہ بیویوں کے لئے روکتے تھے اور عطا کرتے تھے۔ اور جس سال آپ کا وصال ہوا اس سال ازواج مطہرات سے باقی نفقه واپس لینے کا ثبوت نہیں ہے اس لئے باقی نفقہ بیوی کے پاس رہے گا ۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے۔ قال عمر فاني احدثكم عن هذا ... فكان رسول الله ينفق على اهله نفقة سنتهم من هذا المال (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب جبس الرجل قوت سید علی اہلہ وکیف نفقات العيال ؟ ص ۸۰۶ نمبر ۵۳۵۷) اس حدیث میں بیوی کو سال بھر کا نفقہ دینے کا ثبوت ہے۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ نفقہ دینے کے بعد شوہر جب تک زندہ رہا اس کا حساب کیا جائے گا۔ مثلا سال بھر کا نفقہ دیا اور چھ ماہ کے بعد شوہر کا انتقال ہو گیا تو باقہ چھ ماہ کا نفقہ واپس لے گا۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ نفقہ احتباس کا بدلہ ہے۔ اس لئے جتنے دنوں احتباس رہا اتنے دنوں کا نفقہ ساقط ہوگا اور جتنے دنوں کا احتباس نہیں رہا اتنے دنوں کا نفقہ واپس لیا جائے گا۔
(25) And if he advanced her maintenance for a year and then died, he does not recover any of it. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Her maintenance is calculated for what passed, and what remains is for the husband. (Urdu: If he gave advance maintenance for a year then dies, he will not take back anything. Imam Muhammad said: Her maintenance is calculated for the past and the remainder is for the husband. Example: husband gave a year's maintenance, then died after six months; the remaining six months' maintenance is not taken back, it stays with the wife. Reason: wife's maintenance is a gift (silah) and a present; after giving, she becomes owner, so not taken back. Hadith: The Prophet used to give his wives annual maintenance from this wealth... This hadith supports giving a year's maintenance. Imam Muhammad argues that maintenance is compensation for 'ihtibas (confinement), so only the days of confinement are deducted, and the rest is returned.)
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This lesson deals with advance payment of maintenance for a full year and the husband's death during that year. The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) holds that once the husband gives annual maintenance, it becomes the wife's property entirely; if he dies before the year ends, his heirs cannot recover the unused portion. The rationale: nafaqah is a gift (hibah), and a gift is owned upon delivery. A proof is cited from the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who gave annual maintenance and no recovery was reported in the year of his death. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees: he considers maintenance compensation for 'ihtibas (the wife's being bound to the marriage). Therefore, it is like a wage: the wife deserves only for the days she lived with him; the rest is a debt to the husband's estate. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no refund.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, here paid in advance as a lump sum.
احتباس (ihtibas): The wife's confinement to marriage, which is the basis for maintenance according to Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what happens to advance annual maintenance if the husband dies before the year ends?
A) The wife must return the unused portion to the husband's heirs.
B) The wife keeps all of it; no refund is due.
C) The wife may keep only what she has already spent.
D) The qadi decides based on the circumstances.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, advance maintenance is a gift, not a loan; therefore, upon the husband's death, the wife is not required to refund any unused portion.

Maintenance of a Slave Married to a Free Woman
Hanafi ruling on maintenance obligation of a slave married to a free woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maintenance obligation of a slave married to a free woman and explain how it is enforced through sale.

Core Idea
A slave's wife's maintenance is a personal debt; if unpaid, he can be sold.

This passage addresses a specific marital scenario: a slave man marries a free woman. The Hanafi school rules that his maintenance obligation to her becomes a debt (dayn) on his person, and if he cannot pay, he may be sold to settle it. This ruling hinges on the master's permission for the marriage. Let's examine the details and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and obligation
The subject is a slave ('abd) who marries a free woman (hurrah). The obligation is her maintenance (nafaqah).
2
Classify the legal nature
The maintenance is a debt (dayn) on the slave's person, not on his master. It is wajib on the slave.
A debt secured against the slave's person means he can be sold to pay it.
3
Note the condition
The master's permission (idhn) for the marriage is essential. Without it, the sale might not be permitted.
The text explicitly mentions 'since the master permitted the marriage'.
4
Understand enforcement
If the slave cannot pay, the qadi can order him sold to satisfy the debt. This is the remedy for the wife.
5
Compare with other schools
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may not allow selling a slave for such personal debts. This is a point of ikhtilaf.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۵۳](۲۶) واذا تزوج العبد حرة فنفقتها دين عليه يباع فيها

(۲۶) اگر غلام آزاد عورت سے شادی کرے تو اس کا نفقہ غلام پر دین ہوگا اس میں وہ بیچا جا سکتا ہے۔ ر غلام نے آزاد عورت سے شادی کی۔ اب اس کا نفقہ اس کے ذمے واجب ہو گا لیکن چونکہ اس کے پاس کوئی پیسہ نہیں ہے، جو کچھ ہے وہ مولی کا مال ہے۔ اس لئے نفقہ اس کے ذمہ دین ہوگا۔ اور اگر ادا نہ کر سکا تو اس میں وہ بیچا جائے گا۔
چونکہ مولی کی اجازت سے غلام نے شادی کی ہے اس لئے نفقہ میں وہ بیچا جا سکتا ہے۔
(26) And if a slave marries a free woman, her maintenance is a debt on him, and he may be sold for it. (Urdu: If a slave marries a free woman, her maintenance becomes a debt on him, and he can be sold for it. Because he has no money of his own, all he has belongs to his master. Therefore maintenance is a debt on him, and if he cannot pay, he can be sold. Since the master permitted the marriage, the slave may be sold for maintenance.)
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This is a direct ruling about a slave who marries a free woman. The slave's maintenance obligation for his wife is a debt (dayn) binding on him personally, even though he owns no property. If he cannot pay, he may be sold to satisfy the debt. The reasoning: because the master gave permission for the marriage, the slave's person can be sold just as any other asset to fulfill his obligations. This distinguishes the Hanafi position from some other schools that may not allow selling a slave for maintenance debts.

Key Terms
دین (dayn): A debt that is binding on the slave's person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this Hanafi ruling, what can happen if a slave who married a free woman fails to pay her maintenance?
A) The master must pay.
B) The slave can be sold to cover the debt.
C) The wife has no recourse.
D) The marriage is automatically annulled.

Key Takeaway
A slave married to a free woman with his master's permission is personally liable for her maintenance as a debt, and may be sold to satisfy it.

Maintenance of a Free Man Married to a Slave Woman
Hanafi ruling on conditional maintenance for a slave wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a free man's maintenance for his slave wife becomes wajib, and identify when it is not due.

Core Idea
Maintenance for a slave wife is wajib only after the master sends her to the husband.

Marriage alone does not automatically obligate a free husband to support a slave wife. The obligation hinges on the master's act of sending her to live with him. This lesson focuses on that condition so you can apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is providing maintenance (nafaqah) to a slave wife. The ruling is conditional wajib.
2
Identify the condition that makes it wajib
The master must send the slave woman to reside with the husband. Only then is maintenance due.
The Arabic phrase: 'فبرأها مولاها' means the master sends her to stay.
3
Identify when the obligation does not arise
If the master does not send her, no maintenance is due on the husband, even if the marriage contract is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۵۴] (۲۷) واذا تزوج الرجل امة فبرأها مولاها

(۲۷) اگر آدمی نے باندی سے شادی کی ، پس اس کے مولی نے اس کے ساتھ ٹھہرنے کے لئے بھیج دیا تو اس پر اس کا نفقہ ہے۔ اگر
نہیں ٹھہرایا تو اس کے لئے اس پر نفقہ نہیں ہے۔
(27) And if a man marries a slave woman, and her master sends her to stay with him, then her maintenance is on him. If not, then no maintenance is due on him. (Urdu: If a man marries a slave girl, and her master sends her to live with him, then maintenance is due on him. If the master does not send her, then no maintenance is due on him.)
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This lesson deals with the maintenance of a slave wife (umm walad or amah) when her husband is a free man. The ruling is conditional: maintenance is only due on the husband if the master (owner) of the slave woman actually sends her to live with the husband. If the master retains her in his own service or otherwise does not send her to the husband's house, the husband has no maintenance obligation. This is because the husband does not have full marital access (tamkin) unless the wife is physically with him, and maintenance is tied to availability for marital relations.

Key Terms
تمکین (tamkin): The wife's availability for marital relations, a prerequisite for maintenance.
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a free man become obligated to provide maintenance to his slave wife according to this passage?
A) Immediately upon marriage.
B) Only if the master sends her to live with him.
C) Only if he has sufficient wealth.
D) Only after the master grants her freedom.

Key Takeaway
A free man's maintenance for his slave wife is only wajib if the master sends her to live with him.

Maintenance of Minor Children: The Father's Exclusive Obligation
Ruling establishing father's exclusive obligation for child support

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance of minor children, cite the primary proofs from Quran and hadith, and explain why the father's obligation is exclusive.

Core Idea
The father alone bears the wajib of maintaining his minor children; no one shares this duty.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: the financial responsibility for minor children rests solely on the father. The author draws an analogy to the wife's maintenance to emphasise exclusivity, and then provides two primary proofs: the Qur'anic verse (2:233) and the hadith of Hind bint Utbah. By the end, you will understand not only the ruling but also its evidentiary basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening statement: 'And the maintenance of minor children is upon the father; no one shares with him in it.' Determine the hukm level (fard, wajib, etc.).
The term used is 'upon' (على), indicating an obligation.
2
Analyse the Analogy
The author compares child maintenance to wife's maintenance. Why does he use this analogy? What does it reveal about the nature of the father's duty?
The analogy reinforces exclusivity: just as no one shares in wife's nafaqah, no one shares in children's nafaqah.
3
Examine the Quranic Proof
Find the direct citation of Quran 2:233. How does this verse support the father's obligation? Consider the wording and context.
The verse explicitly places provision and clothing 'upon the father' (وعلى المولود له).
4
Examine the Hadith Proof
Review the hadith of Hind. What did the Prophet (ﷺ) permit her to do? How does this establish the obligation on the father?
The Prophet's permission to take from Abu Sufyan's wealth shows that child maintenance is his duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفقة الاولاد الصغار على الاب لا يشاركه فيها احد كما لا يشاركه فى نفقة الزوجة احد [۲۱۵۵] (۲۸) چھوٹی اولاد کا نفقہ باپ پر ہے اس میں کوئی شریک نہ ہو جیسے کہ شوہر شریک نہیں کرتا بیوی کے نفقے میں کسی کو تشریح جیسے بیوی کے نفقے میں اولاد کو شریک نہیں کرتا بلکہ بیوی کو مستقل نفقہ دیتا ہے اسی طرح چھوٹی اولاد کے نفقے میں کسی کو شریک نہیں کرے گا بلکہ جتنا اس کا نفقہ ہوتا ہے وہ اولاد کومستقل طور پر دے گا وجہ آیت میں ہے کہ اولاد کا نفقہ باپ پر واجب ہے۔ وعلی المولود لہ رزقتهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف آیت ۲۳۳ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) (۲) اور حدیث میں ہے۔ ان ھند بنت عتبة قالت يا رسول الله ان ابا سفیان رجل شحيح وليس يعطيني ما يكفيني وولدى الا ما اخذت منه وهو لا يعلم فقال خذی ما یکفیک و ولدک بالمعروف (ب)(بخاری شریف ، باب اذالم ينفق الرجل فللمرأة ان تأخذ بغير علمه ما یکفیک و ولدها بالمعروف ص ۸۰۸ نمبر ۵۳۶۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ بچے کا نفقہ باپ پر واجب ہے۔ اور بچے کا نفقہ مستقل طور پر واجب ہے تو اس میں کسی کو شریک نہیں کیا جاسکتا۔ ہاں بچے خود دوسرے کو شریک کر لے تو یہ اس کی مرضی ہے۔
And the maintenance of minor children is upon the father; no one shares with him in it, just as no one shares with him in the maintenance of the wife. [2155] (28) The maintenance of minor children is on the father; no one is a partner in it, just as the husband does not make anyone a partner in the maintenance of the wife. Explanation: Just as he does not make children partners in the maintenance of the wife, but rather gives the wife her maintenance independently, similarly in the maintenance of minor children he will not make anyone a partner, but will give the children their maintenance independently. The reason is that the Qur'an says that the maintenance of children is obligatory on the father: 'And upon the father is their provision and clothing according to what is reasonable' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:233). And in the hadith: Hind bint Utbah said, 'O Messenger of Allah, Abu Sufyan is a miserly man and does not give me what suffices me and my child, except what I take from him without his knowledge.' He said, 'Take what suffices you and your child according to what is reasonable.' (Bukhari, Book of Spending, chapter on if a man does not spend, the woman may take without his knowledge what suffices her and her child, p.808 no.5364). This hadith shows that the maintenance of a child is obligatory on the father. And the maintenance of a child is independently obligatory, so no one can be made a partner in it. However, if the child himself makes another a partner, that is his choice.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Book of Maintenance (Kitab al-Nafaqat) establishing that the father alone bears the obligation to provide for his minor children. No one—neither the mother, nor grandparents, nor any relative—shares this duty with him. The author draws an analogy to the wife's maintenance, which also belongs exclusively to the husband. The proof is two-fold: first, Qur'an 2:233, which places provision and clothing for children on the father; second, the well-known hadith of Hind bint Utbah, where the Prophet (ﷺ) permitted her to take from her husband's wealth what suffices her and her child, establishing that child maintenance is the father's duty. The commentary explains that the obligation is independent (mustaqill), meaning the father cannot force anyone else to contribute. If the child himself voluntarily allows another to help, that is permissible, but it does not shift the obligation.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance or financial support, including food, clothing, and shelter, obligatory on the father for his minor children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this ruling, who is obligated to provide maintenance for minor children?
A) Both parents equally
B) The father alone
C) The mother alone
D) The grandfather

Key Takeaway
The father alone must maintain his minor children; no one else shares this wajib obligation.
